(January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="S\xc3\xa3o Tom\xc3\xa9 and Pr\xc3\xadncipe"><img alt="S\xc3\xa3o Tom\xc3\xa9 and Pr\xc3\xadncipe" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Flag_of_Sao_Tome_and_Principe.svg/23px-Flag_of_Sao_Tome_and_Principe.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Flag_of_Sao_Tome_and_Principe.svg/35px-Flag_of_Sao_Tome_and_Principe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Flag_of_Sao_Tome_and_Principe.svg/46px-Flag_of_Sao_Tome_and_Principe.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2800" data-file-height="1400" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe_dobra" title="S\xc3\xa3o Tom\xc3\xa9 and Pr\xc3\xadncipe dobra">S\xc3\xa3o Tom\xc3\xa9 and Pr\xc3\xadncipe dobra</a>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia"><img alt="Serbia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Flag_of_Serbia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Serbia.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Flag_of_Serbia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Serbia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Flag_of_Serbia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Serbia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="945" data-file-height="630" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Serbian_dinar" title="Serbian dinar">Serbian dinar</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><a href="/wiki/West_African_CFA_franc" title="West African CFA franc">West African CFA franc</a>\n</li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>euro</b> (<a href="/wiki/Currency_symbol" title="Currency symbol">symbol</a>: <b><a href="/wiki/Euro_sign" title="Euro sign">\xe2\x82\xac</a></b>; <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">code</a>: <b>EUR</b>) is the official currency of 19 of the 27 <a href="/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union" title="Member state of the European Union">member states</a> of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. This group of states is known as the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">eurozone</a> or euro area and includes about 343&#160;million citizens as of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-EC_Euro_Area_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC_Euro_Area-19">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EC_Euro_Area_2_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC_Euro_Area_2-20">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The euro, which is divided into 100 <a href="/wiki/Cent_(currency)" title="Cent (currency)">cents</a>, is the second-largest and second-most traded currency in the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">foreign exchange market</a> after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">United States dollar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The currency is also used officially by the <a href="/wiki/Institutions_of_the_European_Union" title="Institutions of the European Union">institutions of the European Union</a>, by <a href="/wiki/International_status_and_usage_of_the_euro" title="International status and usage of the euro">four European microstates</a> that are not EU members,<sup id="cite_ref-EC_Euro_Area_2_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC_Euro_Area_2-20">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territory" class="mw-redirect" title="British Overseas Territory">British Overseas Territory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_and_Dhekelia" title="Akrotiri and Dhekelia">Akrotiri and Dhekelia</a>, as well as unilaterally by <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>. Outside Europe, a number of <a href="/wiki/Special_member_state_territories_and_the_European_Union" title="Special member state territories and the European Union">special territories of EU members</a> also use the euro as their currency. Additionally, over 200&#160;million people worldwide <a href="/wiki/Euro#Currencies_pegged_to_the_euro" title="Euro">use currencies pegged to the euro</a>.\n</p><p>The euro is the second-largest <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">reserve currency</a> as well as the second-most traded currency in the world after the United States dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\nAs of December&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, with more than  \xe2\x82\xac1.3&#160;trillion in circulation, the euro has one of the highest combined values of banknotes and coins in circulation in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The name <i>euro</i> was officially adopted on 16 December 1995 in Madrid.<sup id="cite_ref-madrid1995_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madrid1995-27">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The euro was introduced to world financial markets as an <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_account" title="Unit of account">accounting currency</a> on 1 January 1999, replacing the former <a href="/wiki/European_Currency_Unit" title="European Currency Unit">European Currency Unit</a> (ECU) at a ratio of 1:1 (US$1.1743). Physical euro coins and banknotes entered into circulation on 1 January 2002, making it the day-to-day operating currency of its original members, and by March 2002 it had completely replaced the former currencies.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> While the euro dropped subsequently to US$0.83 within two years (26 October 2000), it has traded above the U.S. dollar since the end of 2002, peaking at US$1.60 on 18 July 2008 and since then returning near to its original issue rate. In late 2009, the euro became immersed in the <a href="/wiki/European_debt_crisis" title="European debt crisis">European sovereign-debt crisis</a>, which led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/European_Financial_Stability_Facility" title="European Financial Stability Facility">European Financial Stability Facility</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_euro#Reform" title="History of the euro">other reforms</a> aimed at stabilising and strengthening the currency.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Notes"><span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration">Administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/180px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/270px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/360px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3622" data-file-height="6000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a> is <a href="/wiki/Seat_of_the_European_Central_Bank" title="Seat of the European Central Bank">seated</a> in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, Germany, and is in charge of the monetary policy of the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">eurozone</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eurogroup" title="Eurogroup">Eurogroup</a></div>\n<p>The euro is managed and administered by the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>-based <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a> (ECB) and the <a href="/wiki/Eurosystem" title="Eurosystem">Eurosystem</a> (composed of the <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central banks</a> of the eurozone countries). As an independent central bank, the ECB has sole authority to set <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">monetary policy</a>. The Eurosystem participates in the printing, minting and distribution of <a href="/wiki/Euro_banknotes" title="Euro banknotes">notes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euro_coins" title="Euro coins">coins</a> in all member states, and the operation of the eurozone payment systems.\n</p><p>The 1992 <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a> obliges most EU member states to adopt the euro upon meeting certain monetary and budgetary <a href="/wiki/Euro_convergence_criteria" title="Euro convergence criteria">convergence criteria</a>, although not all states have done so. Denmark has negotiated exemptions,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> while Sweden (which joined the EU in 1995, after the Maastricht Treaty was signed) turned down the euro in a <a href="/wiki/2003_Swedish_euro_referendum" title="2003 Swedish euro referendum">non-binding referendum in 2003</a>, and has circumvented the obligation to adopt the euro by not meeting the monetary and budgetary requirements. All nations that have joined the EU since 1993 have pledged to adopt the euro in due course.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Issuing_modalities_for_banknotes">Issuing modalities for banknotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Issuing modalities for banknotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Since 1 January 2002, the national central banks (NCBs) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">ECB</a> have issued euro banknotes on a joint basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Scheller_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scheller-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Eurosystem NCBs are required to accept euro banknotes put into circulation by other Eurosystem members and these banknotes are not repatriated. The ECB issues 8% of the total value of banknotes issued by the Eurosystem.<sup id="cite_ref-Scheller_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scheller-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In practice, the ECB\'s banknotes are put into circulation by the NCBs, thereby incurring matching liabilities vis-\xc3\xa0-vis the ECB. These liabilities carry interest at the main refinancing rate of the ECB. The other 92% of euro banknotes are issued by the NCBs in proportion to their respective shares of the ECB capital key,<sup id="cite_ref-Scheller_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scheller-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> calculated using national share of European Union (EU) population and national share of EU GDP, equally weighted.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coins_and_banknotes">Coins and banknotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Coins and banknotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Euro_coins" title="Euro coins">Euro coins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euro_banknotes" title="Euro banknotes">Euro banknotes</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg/220px-Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg/330px-Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg/440px-Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Euro_coins_and_banknotes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Euro_coins" title="Euro coins">Euro coins</a> and banknotes of various denominations</div></div></div>\n<p>The euro is divided into 100 <a href="/wiki/Cent_(currency)" title="Cent (currency)">cents</a> (also referred to as <i>euro cents</i>, especially when distinguishing them from other currencies, and referred to as such on the common side of all cent coins). In Community legislative acts the plural forms of <i>euro</i> and <i>cent</i> are spelled without the <i>s</i>, notwithstanding normal English usage.<sup id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-32">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise, normal English plurals are used,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> with many <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_issues_concerning_the_euro" class="mw-redirect" title="Linguistic issues concerning the euro">local variations</a> such as <i>centime</i> in France.\n</p><p>All circulating coins have a <i>common side</i> showing the denomination or value, and a map in the background. Due to the <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">linguistic plurality in the European Union</a>, the Latin alphabet version of <i>euro</i> is used (as opposed to the less common Greek or Cyrillic) and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a> (other text is used on national sides in national languages, but other text on the common side is avoided). For the denominations except the 1-, 2- and 5-cent coins, the map only showed the 15 member states which were members when the euro was introduced. Beginning in 2007 or 2008 (depending on the country), the old map was replaced by a map of Europe also showing countries outside the EU like <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The 1-, 2- and 5-cent coins, however, keep their old design, showing a geographical map of Europe with the 15 member states of 2002 raised somewhat above the rest of the map. All common sides were designed by <a href="/wiki/Luc_Luycx" title="Luc Luycx">Luc Luycx</a>. The coins also have a <i>national side</i> showing an image specifically chosen by the country that issued the coin. Euro coins from any member state may be freely used in any nation that has adopted the euro.\n</p><p>The coins are issued in denominations of <a href="/wiki/2_euro_coins" class="mw-redirect" title="2 euro coins">\xe2\x82\xac2</a>, <a href="/wiki/1_euro_coins" class="mw-redirect" title="1 euro coins">\xe2\x82\xac1</a>, <a href="/wiki/50_euro_cent_coin" title="50 euro cent coin">50c</a>, <a href="/wiki/20_euro_cent_coin" title="20 euro cent coin">20c</a>, <a href="/wiki/10_euro_cent_coin" title="10 euro cent coin">10c</a>, <a href="/wiki/5_euro_cent_coin" title="5 euro cent coin">5c</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_euro_cent_coin" title="2 euro cent coin">2c</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1_euro_cent_coin" title="1 euro cent coin">1c</a>. To avoid the use of the two smallest coins, some cash transactions are rounded to the nearest five cents in the Netherlands and Ireland<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> (by voluntary agreement) and in Finland (by law).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This practice is discouraged by the commission, as is the practice of certain shops of refusing to accept high-value euro notes.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/%E2%82%AC2_commemorative_coins" class="mw-redirect" title="\xe2\x82\xac2 commemorative coins">Commemorative coins</a> with \xe2\x82\xac2 face value have been issued with changes to the design of the national side of the coin. These include both commonly issued coins, such as the \xe2\x82\xac2 commemorative coin for the fiftieth anniversary of the signing of the Treaty of Rome, and nationally issued coins, such as the coin to commemorate the <a href="/wiki/2004_Summer_Olympics" title="2004 Summer Olympics">2004 Summer Olympics</a> issued by Greece. These coins are legal tender throughout the eurozone. Collector coins with various other denominations have been issued as well, but these are not intended for general circulation, and they are legal tender only in the member state that issued them.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The design for the <a href="/wiki/Euro_banknotes" title="Euro banknotes">euro banknotes</a> has common designs on both sides. The design was created by the Austrian designer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kalina" title="Robert Kalina">Robert Kalina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-euro96_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euro96-40">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Notes are issued in <a href="/wiki/500_euro_note" title="500 euro note">\xe2\x82\xac500</a>, <a href="/wiki/200_euro_note" title="200 euro note">\xe2\x82\xac200</a>, <a href="/wiki/100_euro_note" title="100 euro note">\xe2\x82\xac100</a>, <a href="/wiki/50_euro_note" title="50 euro note">\xe2\x82\xac50</a>, <a href="/wiki/20_euro_note" title="20 euro note">\xe2\x82\xac20</a>, <a href="/wiki/10_euro_note" title="10 euro note">\xe2\x82\xac10</a>, <a href="/wiki/5_euro_note" title="5 euro note">\xe2\x82\xac5</a>. Each banknote has its own colour and is dedicated to an artistic period of European architecture. The front of the note features windows or gateways while the back has bridges, symbolising links between states in the union and with the future. While the designs are supposed to be devoid of any identifiable characteristics, the initial designs by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kalina" title="Robert Kalina">Robert Kalina</a> were of specific bridges, including the <a href="/wiki/Rialto" title="Rialto">Rialto</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pont_de_Neuilly" title="Pont de Neuilly">Pont de Neuilly</a>, and were subsequently rendered more generic; the final designs still bear very close similarities to their specific prototypes; thus they are not truly generic. The monuments looked similar enough to different national monuments to please everyone.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Europa series, or second series, consists of six denominations and no longer includes the \xe2\x82\xac500 with issuance discontinued as of 27 April 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-Bank_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bank-42">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> However, both the first and the second series of euro banknotes, including the \xe2\x82\xac500, remain legal tender throughout the euro area.<sup id="cite_ref-Bank_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bank-42">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Payments_clearing.2C_electronic_funds_transfer"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Payments_clearing,_electronic_funds_transfer">Payments clearing, electronic funds transfer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Payments clearing, electronic funds transfer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Single_Euro_Payments_Area" title="Single Euro Payments Area">Single Euro Payments Area</a></div>\n<p>Capital within the EU may be transferred in any amount from one state to another. All intra-Union transfers in euro are treated as domestic transactions and bear the corresponding domestic transfer costs.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> This includes all member states of the EU, even those outside the eurozone providing the transactions are carried out in euro.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Credit/debit card charging and ATM withdrawals within the eurozone are also treated as domestic transactions; however paper-based payment orders, like cheques, have not been standardised so these are still domestic-based. The ECB has also set up a <a href="/wiki/Clearing_(finance)" title="Clearing (finance)">clearing system</a>, <a href="/wiki/TARGET" class="mw-redirect" title="TARGET">TARGET</a>, for large euro transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currency_sign">Currency sign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Currency sign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Euro_logo_plus_character.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Euro_logo_plus_character.png/220px-Euro_logo_plus_character.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Euro_logo_plus_character.png/330px-Euro_logo_plus_character.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Euro_logo_plus_character.png/440px-Euro_logo_plus_character.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="301" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Euro_logo_plus_character.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The euro sign; logotype and handwritten</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Euro_sign" title="Euro sign">Euro sign</a></div>\n<p>A special <a href="/wiki/Euro_sign" title="Euro sign">euro currency sign</a> (\xe2\x82\xac) was designed after a public survey had narrowed the original ten proposals down to two. The <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> then chose the design created by the Belgian <a href="/wiki/Alain_Billiet" class="mw-redirect" title="Alain Billiet">Alain Billiet</a>. Of the symbol, the Commission stated<sup id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-32">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Inspiration for the \xe2\x82\xac symbol itself came from the <a href="/wiki/Epsilon" title="Epsilon">Greek epsilon</a> (\xd0\x84)<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;note 6&#93;</a></sup>&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 a reference to the cradle of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">European civilisation</a>&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 and the first letter of the word Europe, crossed by two parallel lines to \'certify\' the stability of the euro.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a></cite></div></blockquote>\n<p>The European Commission also specified a euro logo with exact proportions and foreground and background colour tones.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Placement of the currency sign relative to the numeric amount varies from state to state, but for texts in English the symbol (or the <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a>-standard "EUR") should precede the amount.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_euro" title="History of the euro">History of the euro</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a 2008</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div style="float:left;"><a href="/wiki/Maltese_lira" title="Maltese lira">Maltese lira</a></div>\n</td>\n<td>MTL\n</td>\n<td><span style="float:right;"><span data-sort-value="6999429300000000000\xe2\x99\xa0">0.4293</span></span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002007-07-10-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">10 July 2007</span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002008-01-01-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">1 January 2008</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div style="float:left;"><a href="/wiki/Slovak_koruna" title="Slovak koruna">Slovak koruna</a></div>\n</td>\n<td>SKK\n</td>\n<td><span style="float:right;"><span data-sort-value="7001301260000000000\xe2\x99\xa0">30.126</span></span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002008-07-08-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">8 July 2008</span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002009-01-01-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">1 January 2009</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div style="float:left;"><a href="/wiki/Estonian_kroon" title="Estonian kroon">Estonian kroon</a></div>\n</td>\n<td>EEK\n</td>\n<td><span style="float:right;"><span data-sort-value="7001156466000000000\xe2\x99\xa0">15.6466</span></span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002010-07-13-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">13 July 2010</span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002011-01-01-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">1 January 2011</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div style="float:left;"><a href="/wiki/Latvian_lats" title="Latvian lats">Latvian lats</a></div>\n</td>\n<td>LVL\n</td>\n<td><span style="float:right;"><span data-sort-value="6999702804000000000\xe2\x99\xa0">0.702804</span></span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002013-07-09-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">9 July 2013</span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002014-01-01-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">1 January 2014</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div style="float:left;"><a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_litas" title="Lithuanian litas">Lithuanian litas</a></div>\n</td>\n<td>LTL\n</td>\n<td><span style="float:right;"><span data-sort-value="7000345280000000000\xe2\x99\xa0">3.4528</span></span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002014-07-23-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">23 July 2014</span>\n</td>\n<td><span data-sort-value="000000002015-01-01-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">1 January 2015</span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The euro was established by the provisions in the 1992 <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>. To participate in the currency, member states are meant to meet <a href="/wiki/Euro_convergence_criteria" title="Euro convergence criteria">strict criteria</a>, such as a budget <a href="/wiki/Government_budget_deficit" class="mw-redirect" title="Government budget deficit">deficit</a> of less than 3% of their GDP, a debt ratio of less than 60% of GDP (both of which were ultimately widely flouted after introduction), low inflation, and <a href="/wiki/Interest" title="Interest">interest</a> rates close to the EU average. In the Maastricht Treaty, the United Kingdom and Denmark were granted exemptions per their request from moving to the stage of monetary union which resulted in the introduction of the euro. (For <a href="/wiki/Macroeconomic" class="mw-redirect" title="Macroeconomic">macroeconomic</a> theory, see <a href="#Optimal_currency_area">below</a>.)\n</p><p>The name "euro" was officially adopted in <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a> on 16 December 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-madrid1995_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madrid1995-27">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Belgian <a href="/wiki/Esperantist" class="mw-redirect" title="Esperantist">Esperantist</a> <a href="/wiki/Germain_Pirlot" title="Germain Pirlot">Germain Pirlot</a>, a former teacher of French and history is credited with naming the new currency by sending a letter to then <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_European_Commission" title="President of the European Commission">President of the European Commission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Santer" title="Jacques Santer">Jacques Santer</a>, suggesting the name "euro" on 4 August 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to differences in national conventions for <a href="/wiki/Rounding" title="Rounding">rounding</a> and significant digits, all conversion between the national currencies had to be carried out using the process of <a href="/wiki/Triangulation_(finance)" class="mw-redirect" title="Triangulation (finance)">triangulation</a> via the euro. The <i>definitive</i> values of one euro in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate" title="Exchange rate">exchange rates</a> at which the currency entered the euro are shown on the right.\n</p><p>The rates were determined by the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">Council of the European Union</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;note 7&#93;</a></sup> based on a recommendation from the European Commission based on the market rates on 31 December 1998. They were set so that one <a href="/wiki/European_Currency_Unit" title="European Currency Unit">European Currency Unit</a> (ECU) would equal one euro. The European Currency Unit was an accounting unit used by the EU, based on the currencies of the member states; it was not a currency in its own right. They could not be set earlier, because the ECU depended on the closing exchange rate of the non-euro currencies (principally the <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">pound sterling</a>) that day.\n</p><p>The procedure used to fix the conversion rate between the <a href="/wiki/Greek_drachma" title="Greek drachma">Greek drachma</a> and the euro was different since the euro by then was already two years old. While the conversion rates for the initial eleven currencies were determined only hours before the euro was introduced, the conversion rate for the Greek drachma was fixed several months beforehand.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;note 8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The currency was introduced in non-physical form (<a href="/wiki/Traveller%27s_cheque" title="Traveller&#39;s cheque">traveller\'s cheques</a>, electronic transfers, banking, etc.) at midnight on 1 January 1999, when the national currencies of participating countries (the eurozone) ceased to exist independently. Their exchange rates were locked at fixed rates against each other. The euro thus became the successor to the <a href="/wiki/European_Currency_Unit" title="European Currency Unit">European Currency Unit</a> (ECU). The notes and coins for the old currencies, however, continued to be used as <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">legal tender</a> until new euro notes and coins were introduced on 1 January 2002.\n</p><p>\nThe changeover period during which the former currencies\' notes and coins were exchanged for those of the euro lasted about two months, until 28 February 2002. The official date on which the national currencies ceased to be legal tender varied from member state to member state. The earliest date was in Germany, where the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">mark</a> officially ceased to be legal tender on 31 December 2001, though the exchange period lasted for two months more. Even after the old currencies ceased to be legal tender, they continued to be accepted by national central banks for periods ranging from several years to indefinitely (the latter for Austria, Germany, Ireland, Estonia and Latvia in banknotes and coins, and for Belgium, Luxembourg, Slovenia and Slovakia in banknotes only). The earliest coins to become non-convertible were the Portuguese <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_escudo" title="Portuguese escudo">escudos</a>, which ceased to have monetary value after 31 December 2002, although banknotes remain exchangeable until 2022.</p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurozone_crisis">Eurozone crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Eurozone crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Eurozone_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurozone crisis">Eurozone crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_government-debt_crisis" title="Greek government-debt crisis">Greek government-debt crisis</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008\xe2\x80\x932011 Icelandic financial crisis">2008\xe2\x80\x932011 Icelandic financial crisis</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Government_surplus_or_deficit_(EU-USA-UK).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png/220px-Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png/330px-Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Government_surplus_or_deficit_%28EU-USA-UK%29.png 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="367" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Government_surplus_or_deficit_(EU-USA-UK).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Budget deficit of the eurozone compared to the United States and the UK.</div></div></div>\n<p>Following the U.S. financial crisis in 2008, fears of a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_default" title="Sovereign default">sovereign debt crisis</a> developed in 2009 among investors concerning some European states, with the situation becoming particularly tense in <a href="/wiki/2000s_European_sovereign_debt_crisis_timeline" title="2000s European sovereign debt crisis timeline">early 2010</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Greece" title="Economy of Greece">Greece</a> was most acutely affected, but fellow Eurozone members <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Cyprus" title="Economy of Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Economy of the Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Italy" title="Economy of Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Portugal" title="Economy of Portugal">Portugal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Spain" title="Economy of Spain">Spain</a> were also significantly affected.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> All these countries utilized EU funds except Italy, which is a major donor to the EFSF.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> To be included in the eurozone, countries had to fulfil certain <a href="/wiki/Euro_convergence_criteria" title="Euro convergence criteria">convergence criteria</a>, but the meaningfulness of such criteria was diminished by the fact it was not enforced with the same level of strictness among countries.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit" title="Economist Intelligence Unit">Economist Intelligence Unit</a> in 2011, "[I]f the [euro area] is treated as a single entity, its [economic and fiscal] position looks no worse and in some respects, rather better than that of the US or the UK" and the budget deficit for the euro area as a whole is much lower and the euro area\'s government debt/GDP ratio of 86% in 2010 was about the same level as that of the United States. "Moreover", they write, "private-sector indebtedness across the euro area as a whole is markedly lower than in the highly leveraged <a href="/wiki/Anglosphere" title="Anglosphere">Anglo-Saxon</a> economies". The authors conclude that the crisis "is as much political as economic" and the result of the fact that the euro area lacks the support of "institutional <a href="/wiki/Paraphernalia" title="Paraphernalia">paraphernalia</a> (and mutual bonds of solidarity) of a state".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The crisis continued with S&amp;P downgrading the credit rating of nine euro-area countries, including France, then downgrading the entire <a href="/wiki/European_Financial_Stability_Facility" title="European Financial Stability Facility">European Financial Stability Facility</a> (EFSF) fund.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A historical parallel&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 to 1931 when Germany was burdened with debt, unemployment and austerity while France and the United States were relatively strong creditors&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Liaquat_Ahamed#Lords_of_Finance" title="Liaquat Ahamed">gained attention</a> in summer 2012<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> even as Germany received a <a href="/wiki/Bond_credit_rating#Credit_rating_agencies" title="Bond credit rating">debt-rating</a> warning of its own.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In the enduring of this scenario the euro serves as a mean of quantitative primitive accumulation.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_and_indirect_usage">Direct and indirect usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Direct and indirect usage">edit</a><span data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output font-size:118%; left:2px; top:432px"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#F3D858; style="position:absolute; font-size:118%; left:2px; top:456px"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" euro">Unilaterally adopted</a></div></div>\n</div>\n<div style="position:absolute;bottom:2px;right:2px;"><a href="/wiki/Template:Eurozone_labelled_map_interior" title="Template:Eurozone labelled map interior"><img alt="Desc-i.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Desc-i.svg/10px-Desc-i.svg.png" decoding="async" width="10" height="10" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Desc-i.svg/15px-Desc-i.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Desc-i.svg/20px-Desc-i.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="648" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_usage">Direct usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Direct usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The euro is the sole currency of 19 <a href="/wiki/European_Union_member_states" class="mw-redirect" title="European Union member states">EU member states</a>: Austria, Belgium, Cyprus, Estonia, Finland, France, Germany, Greece, Ireland, Italy, Latvia, Lithuania, Luxembourg, Malta, the Netherlands, Portugal, Slovakia, Slovenia, and Spain. These countries constitute the "<a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">eurozone</a>", some 343&#160;million people in total as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-2013_data_sheet_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2013_data_sheet-64">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With all but one (Denmark) of the remaining EU members obliged to join when economic conditions permit, together with future members of the EU, the <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_the_eurozone" title="Enlargement of the eurozone">enlargement of the eurozone</a> is set to continue. Outside the EU, the euro is also the sole currency of Montenegro and Kosovo and several <a href="/wiki/Microstates_and_the_European_Union" title="Microstates and the European Union">European microstates</a> (Andorra, Monaco, San Marino and the Vatican City) as well as in three <a href="/wiki/Special_member_state_territories_and_the_European_Union" title="Special member state territories and the European Union">overseas territories of France</a> that are not themselves part of the EU, namely (<a href="/wiki/Saint_Barth%C3%A9lemy" title="Saint Barth\xc3\xa9lemy">Saint Barth\xc3\xa9lemy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Pierre_and_Miquelon" title="Saint Pierre and Miquelon">Saint Pierre and Miquelon</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/French_Southern_and_Antarctic_Lands" title="French Southern and Antarctic Lands">French Southern and Antarctic Lands</a>. Together this direct usage of the euro outside the EU affects nearly 3&#160;million people.\n</p><p>The euro has been used as a trading currency in Cuba since 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Syria since 2006,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and Venezuela since 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> There are also various currencies pegged to the euro (see below). In 2009, Zimbabwe abandoned its <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_dollar" title="Zimbabwean dollar">local currency</a> and used major currencies instead, including the euro and the United States dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_reserve_currency">Use as reserve currency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Use as reserve currency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since its introduction, the euro has been the second most widely held international <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">reserve currency</a> after the U.S. dollar. The share of the euro as a reserve currency increased from 18% in 1999 to 27% in 2008. Over this period, the share held in U.S. dollar fell from 71% to 64% and that held in RMB fell from 6.4% to 3.3%. The euro inherited and built on the status of the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">Deutsche Mark</a> as the second most important reserve currency. The euro remains underweight as a reserve currency in advanced economies while overweight in emerging and developing economies: according to the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a><sup id="cite_ref-external1_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-external1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> the total of euros held as a reserve in the world at the end of 2008 was equal to $1.1&#160;trillion or \xe2\x82\xac850&#160;billion, with a share of 22% of all currency reserves in advanced economies, but a total of 31% of all currency reserves in emerging and developing economies.\n</p><p>The possibility of the euro becoming the first international reserve currency has been debated among economists.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Former <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> Chairman <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">Alan Greenspan</a> gave his opinion in September 2007 that it was "absolutely conceivable that the euro will replace the US dollar as reserve currency, or will be traded as an equally important reserve currency".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In contrast to Greenspan\'s 2007 assessment, the euro\'s increase in the share of the worldwide currency reserve basket has slowed considerably since 2007 and since the beginning of the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Credit_crunch" title="Credit crunch">credit crunch</a> related recession and <a href="/wiki/European_debt_crisis" title="European debt crisis">European sovereign-debt crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-external1_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-external1-69">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currencies_pegged_to_the_euro">Currencies pegged to the euro</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Currencies pegged to the euro">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_status_and_usage_of_the_euro" title="International status and usage of the euro">International status and usage of the euro</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg/300px-DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg/450px-DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg/600px-DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="443" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DOLLAR_AND_EURO_IN_THE_WORLD.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worldwide use of the euro and the US dollar: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#092D98; color:white;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a></div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#98b3ff; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;External adopters of the euro</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#510999; color:white;">&#160;</span>&#160;Currencies pegged to the euro</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#CC99FF; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;Currencies pegged to the euro within narrow band</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#099811; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;United States</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#99FF9E; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;External adopters of the US dollar</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#999909; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;Currencies pegged to the US dollar</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#FFFF99; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;Currencies pegged to the US dollar within narrow band</div> <hr /> <small>Note: The <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_ruble" title="Belarusian ruble">Belarusian ruble</a> is pegged to the euro, <a href="/wiki/Russian_ruble" title="Russian ruble">Russian ruble</a> and US dollar in a currency basket.</small></div></div></div>\n<p>Outside the eurozone, a total of 22 countries and territories that do not belong to the EU have currencies that are directly <a href="/wiki/Fixed_exchange_rate_system" title="Fixed exchange rate system">pegged</a> to the euro including 14 countries in mainland Africa (<a href="/wiki/CFA_franc" title="CFA franc">CFA franc</a>), two African island countries (<a href="/wiki/Comorian_franc" title="Comorian franc">Comorian franc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verdean_escudo" title="Cape Verdean escudo">Cape Verdean escudo</a>), three French Pacific territories (<a href="/wiki/CFP_franc" title="CFP franc">CFP franc</a>) and three Balkan countries, Bosnia and Herzegovina (<a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_convertible_mark" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina convertible mark">Bosnia and Herzegovina convertible mark</a>), Bulgaria (<a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_lev" title="Bulgarian lev">Bulgarian lev</a>) and North Macedonia (<a href="/wiki/Macedonian_denar" title="Macedonian denar">Macedonian denar</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-auto_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-18">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> On 28 July 2009, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="S\xc3\xa3o Tom\xc3\xa9 and Pr\xc3\xadncipe">S\xc3\xa3o Tom\xc3\xa9 and Pr\xc3\xadncipe</a> signed an agreement with Portugal which will eventually tie its currency to the euro.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/Moroccan_dirham" title="Moroccan dirham">Moroccan dirham</a> is tied to a basket of currencies, including the euro and the US dollar, with the euro given the highest weighting.\n</p><p>With the exception of Bosnia, Bulgaria, North Macedonia (which had pegged their currencies against the Deutsche Mark) and Cape Verde (formerly pegged to the Portuguese escudo), all of these non-EU countries had a currency peg to the French Franc before pegging their currencies to the euro. Pegging a country\'s currency to a major currency is regarded as a safety measure, especially for currencies of areas with weak economies, as the euro is seen as a stable currency, prevents runaway inflation and encourages foreign investment due to its stability.\n</p><p>Within the EU several currencies are pegged to the euro, mostly as a precondition to joining the eurozone. The <a href="/wiki/Danish_krone" title="Danish krone">Danish krone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatian_kuna" title="Croatian kuna">Croatian kuna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_lev" title="Bulgarian lev">Bulgarian lev</a> are pegged due to their participation in the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">ERM II</a>.\n</p><p>In total, as of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 182&#160;million people in Africa use a currency pegged to the euro, 27&#160;million people outside the eurozone in Europe, and another 545,000 people on Pacific islands.<sup id="cite_ref-2013_data_sheet_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2013_data_sheet-64">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since 2005, stamps issued by the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Military_Order_of_Malta" title="Sovereign Military Order of Malta">Sovereign Military Order of Malta</a> have been denominated in euros, although the Order\'s official currency remains the <a href="/wiki/Maltese_scudo" title="Maltese scudo">Maltese scudo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-smom_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smom-73">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The Maltese scudo itself is pegged to the euro and is only recognised as legal tender within the Order.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optimal_currency_area">Optimal currency area</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Optimal currency area">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Optimum_currency_area" title="Optimum currency area">Optimum currency area</a></div><p>\nIn economics, an optimum currency area, or region (OCA or OCR), is a geographical region in which it would maximise economic efficiency to have the entire region share a single currency. There are two models, both proposed by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mundell" title="Robert Mundell">Robert Mundell</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Optimum_currency_area#OCA_with_stationary_expectations" title="Optimum currency area">stationary expectations model</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Optimum_currency_area#OCA_with_international_risk_sharing" title="Optimum currency area">international risk sharing model</a>. Mundell himself advocates the international risk sharing model and thus concludes in favour of the euro.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> However, even before the creation of the single currency, there were concerns over diverging economies. Before the <a href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Late-2000s recession">late-2000s recession</a> it was considered unlikely that a state would leave the euro or the whole zone would collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> However the <a href="/wiki/Greek_government-debt_crisis" title="Greek government-debt crisis">Greek government-debt crisis</a> led to former <a href="/wiki/British_Foreign_Secretary" class="mw-redirect" title="British Foreign Secretary">British Foreign Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a> claiming the eurozone could not last in its current form.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Part of the problem seems to be the rules that were created when the euro was set up. John Lanchester, writing for <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, explains it: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The guiding principle of the currency, which opened for business in 1999, were supposed to be a set of rules to limit a country\'s annual deficit to three per cent of gross domestic product, and the total accumulated debt to sixty per cent of G.D.P. It was a nice idea, but by 2004 the two biggest economies in the euro zone, Germany and France, had broken the rules for three years in a row.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transaction_costs_and_risks">Transaction costs and risks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Euro&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Transaction costs and risks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable" style="margin-left:10px; margin-bottom:10px;">\n<caption>Most traded currencies by value<br /><small>Currency distribution of global foreign exchange market turnover</small><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Rank</th>\n<th>Currency</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">ISO 4217</a> code<br />(symbol)</th>\n<th>% of daily trades<br />(bought or sold)<br />(April 2019)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto;
